CATALOGUE
OF THE
INDIGENOUS AND NATURALISED PLANTS
OF
QUEENSLAND.
BY
FREDK. MANSON BAILEY, F.L.S.,
COLONIAL BOTANIST.
BRISBANE:
JAMES C. BEAL, GOVERNMENT PRINTER, WILLIAM STREET.
1890,




PEEP ACE.
Whett the Synopsis of the Queensland Flora was published in 1883,
separate copies of the classified index were also issued in pamphlet
form, and as each succeeding supplement to that work has been
published the same plan has been adopted of printing separate
classified indexes. These have been found very convenient for
ready reference, and the call for them has been so great as to
exhaust the supply of the first part. It has, therefore, been deemed
advisable to publish on the same plan a full classified catalogue of the
plants of Queensland, including also those which have become
naturalised; hence the present work.
It is the plan of some botanists, when enumerating the plants of a
country, to omit all varieties as well as naturalised plants. In the first
case this is misleading, for whether a plant is ranked as a species or
variety is entirely at the option of the arranger; and with regard to the
naturalised plants of this colony, many of these have taken so kindly to
the soil and climate that one requires to be well conversant with the
flora to distinguish them from purely indigenous species.
This opportunity is taken of soliciting persons, and especially
those residing or travelling in the more unsettled parts, to collect and
forward to the writer shoots bearing flowers or seeds of the various
plants they may meet with. By this means not only the range of a
species is made known, but frequently new ones are discovered. As
an instance of the large number of new plants that remain unknown in
Queensland, it may be mentioned that over sixty species have been
added to the known flora by the recent expedition to the summit of
Bellenden-Ker, and yet the whole of that collection has not been
finally determined.
June , 1890.
F. M. B.

CLASS I.—DICOTYLEDONS.
[An asterisk denotes that the plant is an introduction become more or less naturalised but not
indigenous. Figures denote the Supplements of the Synopsis of the Queensland Flora in
which the plant has been noticed, thus— l, Ist Supplement; 2, 2nd Supplement; 3, 3rd
Supplement. A dagger before the number, thus +3, denotes that the plant is further
described. The unmarked plants will be found in the first part of the Synopsis.]

SECOND ADDENDA
TO
THIRD SUPPLEMENT OF SYNOPSIS OF QUEENSLAND FLORA.
Since the publication of the Third Supplement the plants noticed
below came to band, and are placed here in preference to waiting for
the issue of another supplement.
Order POLYGALEiE.
POLYGALA, Linn.
P. stenoclada, Benth., PI. Austr. i. 141 ; F. v. M., Yict. Nat.,
July, 1890. A slender, glabrous, erect annual, simple or little-
branched. Leaves distant, very narrow-linear, almost terete, obtuse
or minutely pointed, |to 1 in. (. Muell. , attaining 2 in.) long. Ped-
uncle lateral, slender, elongated, bearing towards the top a slender
raceme of small blue flowers on short pedicels (Muell., |-in. long).
Outer sepals lanceolate, very acute, with scarious margins; inner
sepals about 2 lines long, broadly ovate-lanceolate and falcate with
dark-coloured point. Keel-petal towards the summit blue, and cleft
into only a few lobes, which are thickish; the free part of the fila-
ments conspicuously longer than the anthers. Ovary glabrous. Style
slender, much curved, with an almost petaloid deflexed blue stigma,
bearded on the under side. Capsule with rounded blunt lobes but no
marginal wing.— Benth. et F. v. M., l.c.
P. S. var.? stenosepala, Benth., PI. Austr. i. 141, and F. v. M.,
Yict. Nat., July, 1890. Rather taller and more branched. Leaves
oblong, oblong or linear, flat, fto 1 in. long. Plowers in a loose
pedunculate raceme, pedicels shorter; inner sepals narrow, pointed,
much falcate. Keel-petal more fringed and pink, capsule broader.
Hab.: Various localities in Tropical Queensland. — F. v. M.
Order STERCULIACEA2.
Tribe DOMBEYJE.
MELHANIA, Porsk.
M. abyssinica, A. Rich., PI. Abyss, i. 76, t. 18. Stock woody,
tortuous, divided above into a number of crowded, subcaespitose, erect
branches, the latter covered with greyish down. Leaf-stalks less than
o


an inch long, shorter than the subcordate, oval, obtuse, crenate-dentate
leaves, which are unicostate, downy on both surfaces, paler beneath.
Stipules hair-like. Peduncles axillary, equal to or exceeding the
petioles, simple or bifurcate. Buds obloDg, cylindrical. Blowers
when expanded f-in. across. Epicalyx 1-sided, of 3 linear-subulate
braeteoles as long, or nearly so, as the ovate-lanceolate downy
sepals. Petals yellow, convolute. Style short. Capsule roundish or
slightly pointed, villose, slightly exceeding the persistent calyx. Seeds
punctate, tuberculate.— Mast, in Oliv. PI. Tx*op. Afr. i. 231.
Brotera ovata, Cav. Ic. v. 20, t. 433; B. Leprieurii , Guill. et
Perr., PI. Seneg. i. 85 ; Melhania ovata , Boiss., Fi. Orient, i. 841; M.
Leprieurii , Webb, FI. Nigrit. 110, t. 4, 5.
Hab. : Near Westwood Station, Central Railway.— Dr. T. P. Lucas.
The small fragmentary specimen sent t o me by Dr. Lucas for determination
was certainly belonging to the above plant, but not sufficient to point out any
distinctive form, neither can it as yet be stated with certainty whether it is
indigenous or only an introduction.

Order BURSERACE®.
CANARIUM, Linn.
C. Muelleri (n. sp.) The Queensland Elemi tree. Tree of
medium size, say 50 to 60 ft.,. diameter of stem about 1 ft.,
buds and quite young shoots rusty-tomentose, the smaller branches
with prominent lenticels. Leaves pinnate, of from 5 to 7 leaflets;
petioles slightly hoary or at length glabrous, angular, more or
less flat on the upper side, 2 or 3 in. long, slender, leaving a scar
on the branch from whence they fall; leaflets glabrous, lanceolate, 3
or 4 in. long, 1 to 1-j in. wide in the centre, apex blunt, the base
nearly or quite equal-sided, and tapering to a petiolule of about 2 to
4 lines, primary veins rather distant, and smaller ones forming a more
open reticulation than G. australasicum. Inflorescence in slender
racemose panicles in the upper axils, nearly glabrous; buds in distant
clusters, minute, nearly globose, about 1 line long; calyx with 3
minute teeth not half the length of the petals. Petals 3. Stamens 6.
Filaments very short, attached to the edge of a very shallow cup.
Hab.: Johnstone River. — Dr. Thos. L. Bancroft.


Although only shoots bearing the inflorescence in an early stage hare been
examined, this plant differs widely from the only other Australian species, as well as
the other species of which I have descriptions. I therefore purpose giving to it
specific rank.
Upon cutting a log of this tree Dr. Bancroft observed a flow of honey-like
liquid of a turpentine-like odour, some of which has been analysed by Mr. Mar,
Government Analyst, who says —“ The substance in bottle marked ‘ turpentine ’is
a resin resembling elemi in its general chemical characteristics.”
Order OLACXNEiE.
Teibe ICACINEJE.
APODYTES, E. Meyer.
A. brachystylis, F. v. M '., Fragm. ix. 149. Tree of medium size.
Leaves alternate, lanceolate or oblanceolate, 3 or 4 in. long, 1 to If in.
broad, tapering to a petiole of 2 to 4 lines, both sides more or less
glossy, primary veins few and distant, tbe small reticulation very close
and wavy. Inflorescence terminal, axillary, or lateral, in cymes or
racemes about 1 in. long, clothed with short appressed light-brown
hairs. Calyx cup-shaped, with 5 minute teeth. Petals 5, valvate,
inflexed at the point. Stamens 5 ; filaments flat, broadening upwards,
glabrous except at the top, where the anther is surrounded with a
dense mass of scale-like hairs, like in Lasianthera. Style very short,
slightly curved; stigma lateral; ovary large, glabrous. Fruit bluish-green
and glossy, about If in. long, 6 to 8 lines broad, flat, emarginate, with
prominent midrib and 3 branching prominent veins on either side, on
the outer side the midrib in the fresh fruit expands into a soft fleshy
gibbosity of a dirty-white or light-brown colour, in the dried specimens
this part dries to a rather loose roll of skin over the midrib or suture
of the fruit. Seeds flat, lanceolate, f the length of the fruit.
Hab.: Johnstone River. This is in all probability the plant described by Baron
Mueller, 1.c., he only having flowering specimens. The flower I examined was only
a single bud, in which the stamens were probably only staminodia, for I found no
anthers. Thus the flowers may be polygamous.
Order LEGUMINOSiE.
Teibe GALEGE^.
TEPHROSIA, Pers.
T. polyzyga, F. v. M. in Benth. FI. Austr. ii. 206. (So named
from the numerous pairs of leaflets.) Stems or branches elongated,
hoary or almost silky with a close tomentum. Leaflets 20 to 40 or
more, 2 to 5 lines long, oblong, obtuse, or more or less cuneate, clothing
the rhachis from base to point, the primary veins distant, anastomosing
within the margin, whole leaf about 6 in. long. Flowers small, in
distant clusters, in long often branched racemes. Pedicels short.
Calyx silky-tomentose, 2 to 2f lines long, the lobes rather broad, acute,


shorter than the tube. Standard broad; keel obtuse. Style flat. Pod
nearly straight, to 2in long, softly tomentose. Seeds lenticular.
—j ßenth., l.c.
The Queensland specimens are in early bud and the racemes almost
filiform.
Hab. : Charters Towers.— C. F. Plant.
MILLETTIA, W. et Arn.
HE. pilipes (n. sp.) (So named from the hairy pedicels). A tall
woody climber, glabrous except the inflorescence. Leaves alternate,
pinnate, of 2 or more pairs of pinnae ; pinnae oblong-lanceolate, about
2 in. long, the margins undulate; petiolules rugose, 2or 3 lines
long ; primary veins few, distant, and with the smaller reticulations
rather prominent on both faces. Inflorescence in terminal race-
mose panicles of brownish-pink flowers, the main and secondary
rhachises downy. Bracts ovate-lanceolate, about £-in. long ; pedicels
slender, about f-in. long, clothed with light-brown spreading hairs;
bracteole narrow-linear at top of pedicel. Calyx about lines long,
campanulate, nearly glabrous ; lobes attenuated, about as long as the
cup, the two inner ones joined into one; margins ciliate. Standard
nearly orbicular, about £-in. diameter, with a callous appendage
at the top of the claw; claw short, slightly hairy on the back,
the wing and keel petals coherent at the obtuse points, with sharp
auricles at the base; claws slender. Stamens diadelphous, upper
one free. Ovary tomentose, nearly or quite sessile. Style glabrous,
incurved; stigma small; disk a crenulate, glabrous, short sheath.
Pod not seen.
Hab.: Jobnstone River.— Dr. Thos. L. Bancroft.
Differs from M. megasperma , F. v. M., the only other species of the genus met
with in Australia—which, with Stephan Endlicher’s Norfolk Island Pterocarpus
australis , is given by Baron Mueller in Second Syst. Cens. Austr. Plants, 68, under
Wistaria, of this neither of the authors having seen pods —in bracts and bracteoles
more prominent, shorter and more dense panicles, the more hirsute pedicels, the
joined calyx-lobes not being blunt, the standard not hoary, and probably the pod
may give a further distinction,
Order MYRTACE^.
Tbibe LEPTOSPERMEAE.
CALIISTEMON, R. Br.
C. brachyandrus, Lindl. in Journ. Hort. Soc. iv. 112 ; El. Austr.
iii. 122. A tall, stiff, bushy shrub or small tree, the young shoots
softly hairy, and sometimes soft, loose, spreading hairs persistent on
the older branches and foliage. Leaves linear-subulate, terete and
channelled above, rigid and pungent-pointed, mostly £ to 1£ in. long,
the rhachis and calyxes loosely hairy. Calyx-tube broadly campanulate,


1 to 1| line long ; lobes broad, ciliate, more or less scarious. Petals
about H line diameter, glabrous or pubescent. Stamens quite free
and scarcely above 4 lines long; filaments deep-red; anthers yellow
or pale.— jßenth., l.c.
Hab.: Baron Mueller writes me that he has received specimens of this plant
from Queensland.
XANTHOSTEMON, E. v. M.
X. oppositifolius (n. sp.) (Opposite-leaved Xanthostemon.)
Luya’s Hardwood. Penda of natives; Honer, Irontree, or Redheart.
A large tree, glabrous except the inflorescence and perhaps the early
growth, forming a barrel of 40 to 50 ft., with a diameter up to 4 ft.;
wood hard and durable. Leaves opposite or here and there very rarely
alternate-ovate-oblong, very obtuse, slightly tapering at the base, 2 to
4 in. long, 1 to 2| in. broad, rather thick, on petioles of from i to 1 in.
long, the primary veins rather close and parallel, joining far within the
margin, the finer reticulation close and prominent in the dried leaf,
oil-dots small but numerous, hidden to the unassisted eye by the thick
texture of the leaf. Peduncles to 1 in. long in the upper axils,
usually bearing a cyme of 3 or 4 pedicellate flowers. Bracts small,
falling off from the early inflorescence. (No flowers collected.) Capsule
nearly globular, about diameter, seated on the base of the
expanded 4 or 5-blunt-lobed calyx, opening loculicidally in 3 hard
valves, the epicarp usually separating from the endocarp in each valve,
the placenta prominent and spuriously dividing the cells. Seeds
numerous, flatly compressed.
Hab.: Noosa. This new species has the fruit of X. chrysanthus, and
principally differs from that species in the position and form of foliage. The
finding of this species makes it advisable to follow Baron Mueller and allow the
genus Xanthostemon to merge into the older genus Metrosideros. Mr. A. F.
Luya, from whom I received specimens of the tree, says that “ the wood is
indestructible except by fire, and is suitable for all kinds of bridge-building,
for piles, girders, headstocks, &c., or in fact any work where great strength and
durability are essential.”
Order COMPOSITE.

BRACHYCOME, Cass.
B. chrysoglossa, F. v. M., Trans. Phil. Yict. i. 44.; Yict. PI. i. 305.
(Included in B. margmata in El. Austr. iii. 519.) A perennial desert

Order AMARANTACE^E.
Teibe AMARANTEiE.
TRICIIINIUM, R. Br.
Seeies Polycephala.
T. helipteroides, F. v. M., Eragm. iii. 122; El. Austr. v. 231.
Apparently annual, with several erect or decumbent simple or branched
stems of 6 in. to 1 ft., clothed as well as the foliage with silky-woolly
hairs. Leaves linear or linear-lanceolate, the lower ones petiolate,
1 to 2 in. long, the upper ones nearly sessile and smaller, not
numerous. Spikes at first globular, at length ovoid, fto 1 in. long.
Bracts and bracteoles ovate, thin and transparent, the bracts acute and

more obtuse. Perianth 3i to 4 lines long, the united disk very short,

narrow and rather rigid, the dorsal hairs very fine and not dense, the
inner ones rather shorter and more acute than the outer. Filaments
much dilated at the base, but scarcely united above the disk,
surrounded by woolly hairs not very copious proceeding from the base
of the inner segments, unequal, one or two of the shortest without
anthers.— F. v. M., l.c.

Order CHENOPODIACEiE.

CHENOPODIUM, Linn.
Section Orthosporum.
C. simulans, F.v.M. (Dysphania simulans, E. v. M. et Tate, in
Trans. Boy. Soc. South Austr. viii. 71.) An erect plant resembling
D. plantaginella, E. v. M., stemless or very short stems or branches

2, subtle-capillary; fruit spherical, pale-brown, smooth, translucent;
embryo nearly semicircular.— F. v. 3L., l.c.

Tribe ATBIPLICEtE.
ATRIPLEX, Linn.
A. velutinella, F. v. 31., Ecp. Babb. Exped. 20; El. Austr. v.
174. Apparently herbaceous and procumbent, with elongated
branching stems, the whole plant scaly-tomentose. Leaves sessile or
rarely the lower ones contracted into a short broad petiole, ovate or
rhomboid, coarsely sinuate-toothed, mostly above 1 in. long, or
the upper ones oblong and nearly entire, much smaller, and passing
into small floral bracts. Elowers monoecious, in sessile clusters, the
upper ones with numerous males surrounded by several females, either
in the upper axils or 2 to 6 of the last clusters forming an interrupted
terminal spike ; the lower axillary clusters female. Fruiting perianths
flat, triangular-ovate, about 3 lines long, the hardened base very short
and broad; the valves almost acute, entire or with a few short teeth
on each side at the base, herbaceous, scaly-tomentose, free almost to
the base. Seeds broadly orbicular; the radicle inferior or lateral.—
F. v. M ., l.c.
Hab.: Baron Mueller writes me that lie lias received specimens of this plant
from Queensland.

3. — F. v. M., l.c.

X. Ciliata, F. v. M., [Bep. Babb. Exped. 20; FI. Austr. v. 188.

Order LAURINEJE.

CASSYTHA, Linn.
C. melantha, B. Br. t FI. Austr. v. 311. Stems glabrous, stouter
and flowers larger than in any other species. Spikes very short and

Order EUPHORBIACEiE.
Tbibe CROTONThE.
CROTON, Linn.
C. tomentellus, F. v. M., Fragm. iv. 141; FI. Austr. vi. 127. A
shrub, the smaller branches, foliage, and inflorescence hoary with a
short, close, stellate tomentum, nearly the same on both Bides of the
leaves. Leaves ovate, broadly elliptical, or ovate-oblong, obtuse or
shortly acuminate, entire, 2 to 4 in. long, on petioles of f to above
1 in. long, the glands at fflie base of the lamina usually shortly stipi-
tate. Racemes 2to 6 in. long, with 1 or more female in most


of the lower and middle clusters, the upper clusters entirely males.
Stamens about 10, with glabrous filaments. Female flowers on pedicels
of Ito 2 lines. Calyx-segments scarcely 1 line long, and not enlarged
under the fruit. Styles deeply divided into 2 bifid branches. Capsule
rather depressed, tridymous, 3 to 4 lines broad, tomentose or shortly
hirsute with stellate hairs. Carunculus of the seeds small— Benth., FI.
Austr., l.c.
The description drawn up from imperfect specimens collected by F. v. Mugll. at
the Victoria River ; and leaf specimens collected by A. Cunningham at Careening
Bay, North-west Coast. &
Hab.: Musgrave Telegraph Station, Cape York Peninsula.— T. Barclay-Millar.
These latter specimens differ from the above in the smaller leaves, which are
subrotund, about 1 in. diameter, with crenate margins, triplinerved and prominently
reticulate, the glands at base of lamina sessile and yellow, resembling minute Pezizas.
Racemes about 2 in. long, the flowers not clustered, about 12 stamens in the
males, calyx-segments and petals hairy, styles purple, and so deeply divided or
slightly joined at the base as to appear quite free. No note was sent with the few
specimens as to the growth : it has the appearance of a plant of straggling habit
growing among rocks. Probably, when better specimens are obtained, this Queensland
plant may prove distinct from the Victoria River species.
Order CYPERACEiE.
Tribe EHYNCHOSPORE^.
ELYNANTHUS, Nees.
Spikelets clustered in a narrow panicle, with usually 2 hermaph-
rodite flowers close together on a short not flexuose rhachis, both
fertile or one sterile. Glumes distichous, 2to 4 outer ones empty and
a small empty one close above the flowers. No hypogynous bristles.
(Stamens 3to 8. Style slender, with a thickened hard persistent base
continuous with the ovary ; stigmatic branches 3 or 4, filiform. Nut
ovoid, crowned by the hard ovoid or oblong persistent base of the
style, sometimes as big as itself and either continuous with it or
slightly contracted under it. Perennials with the habit of the narrow
paniculate Schoeni. — Benth. in FI. Austr. vii. 376.
X!. capillaceus, Benth., FI. Austr. vii. 377. Stems filiform, 9
to 18 in. long, leafless except a rather long sheath at the base, either
truncate or with a setaceous lamina or point. Panicle small and
narrow, the spikelets 3 or 4 together in the axil of the lower sheathing
bract, 1 or 2 in the upper ones, the bracts with short subulate points.
Spikelets very narrow, to 2 lines long. Glumes acute or acuminate,
3or 4 outer empty ones rather shorter. Flowers usually only one
fertile. Stamens 3. Nut ovoid, crowned by the ovoid persistent base
of the style as long as itself, as in Caustis.—Benth., l.c.

Minute creeping mosses, sparingly branched, monoecious. Leaves

below it, grooved, split at the side. — Hook., N. Z. FI. 456.
